  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_02626d7b-629f-429b-95e5-5bded416f433'
      });
    }

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_17717035" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/17717035" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/17717035?book_show_action=true">Mar 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780590427104" href="/book/show/393156.The_Girl_with_the_Silver_Eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=kiddiwinx">kiddiwinx</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=mg-rereads-2017">mg-rereads-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17717035" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3683025121058548985"><b>JUNE</b><br/><br/>as part of my personal reading challenges for 2017, once a month i will be revisiting a favorite book from when i was a little bitty karen and seeing if it holds up to my fond memories and determining if i can still enjoy it as an old and crotchety karen.<br/><br/>fingers crossed.<br/><br/>so: first things first. in answer to the question 'does this book hold up?' this time, i gotta say "no." i'm keeping baby karen's star rating, since there's no way to preserve the two ratings, but adult-karen rates this lower</span>
  <span id="freeText3683025121058548985" style="display:none"><b>JUNE</b><br><br>as part of my personal reading challenges for 2017, once a month i will be revisiting a favorite book from when i was a little bitty karen and seeing if it holds up to my fond memories and determining if i can still enjoy it as an old and crotchety karen.<br><br>fingers crossed.<br><br>so: first things first. in answer to the question 'does this book hold up?' this time, i gotta say "no." i'm keeping baby karen's star rating, since there's no way to preserve the two ratings, but adult-karen rates this lower. unless there is a sequel to this book (and there is NOT), baby-karen imagined a whole bunch more to this book than ms. roberts actually wrote. <br><br>baby-karen's review: <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485371647i/21809047._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>this book is great because she has SILVER EYES and that's so cool! also, she can throw rocks at people using her MIND, and i want to do that, too. also, i could set the dinner table while reading in my room and that would be rad. i like to read.<br><br>(it should be noted that baby-karen thought willo davis roberts was a man, probably because "david" and "robert" are typically names given to males, and baby-karen had some mild dyslexia when it came to names - for years, she introduced herself as "karen brissette tiffany." what a dope.)<br><br>adult-review:<br><br>first, here is a detailed, medium-snarky synopsis - you decide if you wanna risk being spoiled on a 198-page book for kids written in 1980. <br><br>katie is a silver-eyed nine-year-old with magical powers that allow her to move things with her miiiiiind!!<br><br>she has been living with her grandmother for years because her parents split up and couldn’t afford to care for her, but then her grandmother died and now she’s living with her mom and they barely know each other and her mom’s bf smokes and doesn’t seem to like kids and both of the babysitters her mom hires are lame and katie has a tendency to freak people out because of her eyes and her flat affect and also because she recklessly uses her powers to harass her sitters and this one grumpy dude in her building and she doesn’t have any friends except for the paperboy and the elderly mrs. m. and her cat lobo and oh, yeah, she can talk to cats which is a much cooler power than telekinesis, if you ask me, but no one asks me, and then this man named mr. c moves into her building and starts asking everyone pretty specific questions about katie that no one seems to find suspicious at all and then katie finds out that her mom used to work at a pharmaceutical factory with four other ladies who all became pregnant at the same time and whose kids were all… unusual, so katie decides to track them down so she won’t feel like such an oddball and luckily they all live within a bus ride from her place and she finds them pretty easily and it turns out mr. c. is basically less-magical but more-ambulatory dr. xavier and runs a school for gifted youngsters and he wants them all to live on his campus but they say noooooo and decide to stick together and stay where they’re at and be magical homeschooled friends only occasionally poked at by mr. c.’s people. the end.<br><br>which is basically cutting the story off just when it becomes interesting, right? unless you like your sci-fi novels to be more about searching through mommy's drawers for her friends' birth announcements, cold-calling strangers, slumber parties as camouflage and riding the bus and not so much about what happens when a bunch of mischievous kids with powers get together. <br><br>i’m not sure why i liked this one so much when i was little, because not a whole lot happens. the sci-fi elements are basically just a metaphor for “being different;” katie is a lonely girl who wants friends and when she finds other kids like her, the implication is that now everything is going to be roses for her, but since the book cuts off before any of those roses blossom, we don’t know if having magical abilities is enough of a foundation upon which to build lasting friendships, and we don’t get to see them do anything cool. <br><br>but i do enjoy being unfair to these books and assessing them with modern-day sensibilities. and so:<br><br>the blurb from <i>booklist</i> on the back cover:<br><br><i>”An intriguing idea…Roberts’ smooth writing will lure [readers] right to the end.”</i><br><br>ah, the 80’s. when you could celebrate someone’s ability to “lure” children without some asshole like me coming along to laff about it. this idea of luring children extends to one of my major criticisms in the book - the lack of suspicion towards the character of mr. c. katie is suspicious of him, but for different reasons than “this rando adult male is showing a lot of interest in this little girl. should we perhaps be alarmed? nah. let’s just freely answer all of his questions and see what happens.”<br><br>katie’s mother sits companionably by the pool with mr. c, who is watching katie swim and asking lots of questions about her and she's all “doodly-doo - what a nice, inquisitive man taking an interest in my nine-year-old daughter!”*<br><br>and this is after he’s approached katie on her own, asking for her help moving in. because this book predates the lessons we all learned from <i>Silence of the Lambs</i> about why you never help a stranger move furniture:<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1500154966ra/23305093.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>and because nine-year-olds are known for their abilities in the department of heavy lifting, particularly scrawny and bookish nine-year-old girls in glasses. true, katie has an advantage with her magical powers and all, but that’s not common knowledge, and his attention should probably cause some mild alarm - both his request and his eagerness to be katie’s swim-buddy:<br><br><i>”Listen, are you busy, young lady, or would you help me haul things in tomorrow morning? When I bring my stuff over? I’ll pay you.”<br><br>Katie shrugged. “Sure. Why not. Are you going to swim in the pool?”<br><br>“Not tonight. Maybe tomorrow. Why, you need a swimming partner?”<br>…<br><br>“Tell you what, after we get my junk carried in tomorrow, we’ll go swimming. OK?”</i><br><br>he asks her to carry his JUNK? gross. but hilarious wordplay aside, bribing a little girl into your apartment alone with the promise of slappy-happy bathing suit time is just red flags all over town.<br><br>katie’s grandmother raised her right, and katie knows to avoid certain situations, even if she’s unclear of the reasons why:<br><br><blockquote>…a man had stopped and asked her if she wanted a ride. He was a perfectly nice man, Katie knew he was, and she hadn’t gotten into the car, and the man had simply smiled and driven away. Katie had tried to explain that it was only that he thought she was a long way from home, and it was cold and raining, and he was kind. But Grandma Welker was convinced he was a child molester.<br><br>Katie was a little vague about what child molesters actually <i>did</i>. But she knew it was something unpleasant, and she had sense enough not to get into a car or walk away with a stranger, for heaven’s sake. Grownups told you and told you things, and then they acted as if you didn’t have any brains at all, even when they admitted you were bright.</blockquote> <br><br>katie does use her bright brain, briefly, to ponder whether mr. c has interference on his mind, or whether her mother will <i>think</i> he does, at any rate:<br><br><i>…she wondered uneasily if her mother would agree to that or if the new tenant fell into the category of “strange men” and was therefore to be treated warily.</i><br><br>and her mother, who hasn't been an active mother for years, still knows all about stranger-danger, which is clear from an argument they have where katie is scoffing at the very idea of a nine-year-old needing a sitter:<br><br><blockquote>”…you’re used to living in the country, and it’s different, in the city. All kinds of things can happen-"<br><br>“I know about child molesters and all that,” Katie said with dignity. “And keeping the doors locked and not admitting on the telephone that I’m alone. I’m not <i>stupid.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br>so how does katie’s mother not equate a man’s interrogations and intense interest in her daughter with the dangers of city living and not register his behavior as ‘off?’ is mr. c’s casual assertion that he’s totes <i>not</i> a kiddy-diddler enough to set her mind at ease?<br><br><i>”…I’d asked the little girl to help me haul things in, in the morning, And then it occurred to me that maybe I’d better talk to her folks, first. Make sure it was all right with them. People get ideas, these days, about strange men and little girls.</i><br><br>maybe don’t say “little girls” so much. <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jItz-uNjoZA" rel="nofollow noopener">it comes across as creep city.</a><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1500154966ra/23305094.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>at least mrs. m. has some sense:<br><br><i>”What do you want with a little girl who isn’t even ten years old yet?” Mrs. M. asked. “She isn’t much more than a baby.”</i><br><br>as it happens, he is not a perv, so all of my affronted bluster is unnecessary, but still, people! STILL! it's carelessness like this that leads to lotion being put in baskets.<br><br>more proof that this was written in the wayback comes in the laxity of punishment for prepubescent students carrying weapons into the classroom.<br><br>katie tells a story about what happened to her at her old school with a bully named DERWARD, and the pocketknife he regularly carried to school.<br><br><blockquote>She’d tried to ignore Derward, but after a few minutes of feeling the point of his pocketknife jabbing more and more painfully between her shoulder blades, Katie had used all the force she could muster and turned the thing back away from herself.<br><br>The next thing she knew, Derward was yelling, and there was blood all over his hand and his desk, and when Miss Cottrell came to the back of the room, she was very angry.</blockquote><br><br>wow, she <i>should</i> be angry! a little boy stabbing a little girl with a knife? what a bully! expel that weaponized bully!!<br><br>but it doesn't really play out that way:<br><br><blockquote>Katie remembered standing in front of the principal’s desk, her legs quivering, and being asked for her version of the story.<br><br>What could she say? That she’d used some mental force that nobody else seemed to have to twist the knife against the boy who was jabbing her with it?<br><br>“It was <i>his</i> knife,” Katie said. “He was fooling around with it, poking me.”<br><br>“And so you twisted around and cut him with it?” the principal asked.<br><br>“I jerked away,” Katie said, “and somehow he cut himself. I can still feel where he poked me.”<br><br>The principal looked at the back of her blouse, but he said there was no cut in it. “Do you want the nurse to look at your back and see if there is a mark on your skin?”<br><br>“No,” Katie said. If there was no tear in her shirt, it was unlikely that there’d be a mark on her skin.<br><br>“But it was his own fault.”<br><br>In the end, nothing happened to either of them, Katie or Derward. They were sent back to class, where spelling was all over and the kids were doing arithmetic. But all the kids looked at Katie out of the corners of their eyes.<br><br>Katie still remembered the way the principal and the teacher had looked at her. Not at Derward, but at <i>her</i>.</blockquote><br><br>wow. what an excellent message that sends. katie's mom had the safety of country living all wrong. but in case you think there's no justice, it should be known that one time, this knife-wielding child was in fact punished:<br><br><blockquote>Once he’d locked some girls in an outhouse when they were on a class picnic at a park, and it had been over an hour before anyone heard them yelling and let them out. Derward had been suspended for three days because of that. Not that Derward minded; he had returned to school boasting that his father had taken him fishing for three days.</blockquote><br><br>so - locking some girls in an outhouse (and while we're at it, how did he shepherd multiple girls into that outhouse to begin with, and how big was this outhouse?) is a suspendable offense, but carrying a weapon is all good, so long as the victim is not well-liked. <br><br>that's all i got in me for this reread. not nearly as good as i remember, but i hope i didn't tarnish any of your childhood memories of it.<br><br>* not an actual quote<br><br>******************************************<br>sigh, another book without a bookplate or other adorable baby-karen scrawls. it <i>does</i> have my handy-dandy “books i read” notations, but that’s only a little bit fun. i will be going to my dad’s place soon, and i hope some of my kids’ books will be accessible and will be FULL of bookplates and doodles. <br><br><a href="http://s1131.photobucket.com/user/kettincat/media/flat%20stanley/flat%20stanley003/IMG_2636_zpsdeginctt.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1500158031i/23305283._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo IMG_2636_zpsdeginctt.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>JANUARY:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1877167670" rel="nofollow noopener">wait till helen comes</a><br> <br><b>FEBRUARY:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1905988771" rel="nofollow noopener">the little gymnast</a><br> <br><b>MARCH:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1938876918" rel="nofollow noopener">zucchini</a><br> <br><b>APRIL</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1974169393" rel="nofollow noopener">something queer at the library</a><br><br><b>MAY</b>: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18473476" rel="nofollow noopener">good-bye pink pig</a><br><br><b>JULY:</b> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2075803132" rel="nofollow noopener">the phantom tollbooth</a><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9586743137594813344">Reread for our classics discussion on Friday 8/28! :)<br/><br/>******<br/><br/>One of the first scifi/paranormal books I ever remember reading, and one that still holds up as an adult. I loved the whole idea of a girl with telekinetic powers who doesn't fit anywhere--until she finds out that there is a small group of other kids just like her. Well-written with memorable characters (and a lovely old cat), it's one of the first books that started my love for non-realistic fiction. <br/><br/>This one's a bit obscure, but it's s</span>
  <span id="freeText9586743137594813344" style="display:none">Reread for our classics discussion on Friday 8/28! :)<br /><br />******<br /><br />One of the first scifi/paranormal books I ever remember reading, and one that still holds up as an adult. I loved the whole idea of a girl with telekinetic powers who doesn't fit anywhere--until she finds out that there is a small group of other kids just like her. Well-written with memorable characters (and a lovely old cat), it's one of the first books that started my love for non-realistic fiction. <br /><br />This one's a bit obscure, but it's still in print and available as an ebook, and well worth seeking out.</span>

<div id="review_2147242" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2147242" />
    <a title="Sean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/138600-sean"><img alt="Sean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562511132p2/138600.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2147242?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean" href="/user/show/138600-sean">Sean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">kids and adults who like kid books</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2147242" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6205274156124156860">This was one of my favorite books for a long time. I reread it at regular intervals, and never ceased to thrill every time Katie learned to use her powers in a different and more spectacular way. I identified strongly with Katie, I think, because I was also an extremely precocious child whom grownups found intimidating and inscrutable, and I valued anyone who treated me like a real person instead of a freak or a "genius."</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_166767331" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166767331" />
    <a title="Wendy Darling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling"><img alt="Wendy Darling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379959930p2/2745288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/166767331?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wendy Darling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wendy Darling" href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling">Wendy Darling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781442421707" href="/book/show/9421414-the-girl-with-the-silver-eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=paranormal-general">paranormal-general</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=sci-fi-or-futuristic">sci-fi-or-futuristic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166767331" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9332240531452688905">One of the first scifi/paranormal books I ever remember reading, and one that still holds up as an adult. I loved the whole idea of a girl with telekinetic powers who doesn't fit anywhere--until she finds out that there is a small group of other kids just like her. Well-written with memorable characters, it's the book that started my love for supernatural books. It's a shame the author's other books never really lived up to this one. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review166767331">
  <td colspan="2">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1722501114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2515046325344274189">The little one said it perfectly, as we neared the end of the book: "But, like, it hasn't even <i>started</i> yet!"<br/><br/>While well-written with likable characters, it felt like the first 7/8 of the book was just setting up the story, and then the climax kind of just plopped out like cold Beefaroni into a dirty bowl. <br/><br/>Perhaps we've been spoiled with all the decent paranormal and superhero fiction available nowadays, but we sort of expected more from a tale about a young girl with telekinesis. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_14684407" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14684407" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/212163-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554516888p2/212163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14684407?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/212163-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/212163-susan?shelf=childhood">childhood</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14684407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15486564710042937946"><b>Updated review:</b><br/><br/>Rereading a childhood favorite is risky. Yet this is one of those books that I remembered so fondly that I had to give it another try--once I realized it's back in print! (Or still in print?) Luckily, that gamble paid off. <br/><br/>The story may not be perfect, and the ending left me wanting more, but I had such a good time reading it. The characters came to life on the page and Katie is still a girl I'd want to be my best friend. <br/><br/>As an adult (and an author), I love how <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/83396.Willo_Davis_Roberts' title='Willo Davis Roberts' rel='nofollow noopener'>Willo Davis Roberts</a></span>
  <span id="freeText15486564710042937946" style="display:none"><b>Updated review:</b><br /><br />Rereading a childhood favorite is risky. Yet this is one of those books that I remembered so fondly that I had to give it another try--once I realized it's back in print! (Or still in print?) Luckily, that gamble paid off. <br /><br />The story may not be perfect, and the ending left me wanting more, but I had such a good time reading it. The characters came to life on the page and Katie is still a girl I'd want to be my best friend. <br /><br />As an adult (and an author), I love how <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/83396.Willo_Davis_Roberts" title="Willo Davis Roberts" rel="nofollow noopener">Willo Davis Roberts</a> writes from a child's perspective without infantilizing the character. Katie doesn't think she needs an adult to look after her and she doesn't mind telling people what she thinks. <br /><br />The book holds up well, even as it shows the ways childhood was different in the 1980s--whether you had silver eyes or not. <br /><br /><b>Original review:</b> <br /><br />I admit, I haven't read this book in at least 20 years, probably 4th or 5th grade, but I loved it back then!<br /><br />I think most kids (adults too) relate to stories where the protagonist is 'different' but discovers that not only are the differences a good thing (super powers), but that they may someday find others who are like them.<br /><br />I'm tempted to pick up this book again, but I have such good memories that I'm not sure I want to chance rereading it with grown-up eyes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15486564710042937946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1638411134" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer5821762741146450609"><b>
  <u>2019</u>
</b><br/>Katie is almost 10 years old. She likes to read books, she <i>doesn't</i> need a babysitter and she doesn't like pineapple on pizza. Oh, and she can move things with her mind. She knows people find her unsettling, both grownups and children alike, but she really does try to not upset them (like by moving things telepathically around them - unless it benefits her). <br/><br/>I really like Katie. And, even though her mom isn't the most nurturing lady, I like the dynamic between the two of them. Katie's mom</span>
  <span id="freeText5821762741146450609" style="display:none"><b>
  <u>2019</u>
</b><br />Katie is almost 10 years old. She likes to read books, she <i>doesn't</i> need a babysitter and she doesn't like pineapple on pizza. Oh, and she can move things with her mind. She knows people find her unsettling, both grownups and children alike, but she really does try to not upset them (like by moving things telepathically around them - unless it benefits her). <br /><br />I really like Katie. And, even though her mom isn't the most nurturing lady, I like the dynamic between the two of them. Katie's mom, Monica, really does try. She may not feel the most comfortable around her own daughter, but she's willing to give it a shot. And Katie instantly connects to Mrs. M (who is awesome, btw). I love watching Katie's character grow. Although it's said that in the past, she's had trouble connecting with other people, it was nice watching Katie connect with others, like Mrs. M. And Jackson Jones. Even Miss K likes her.<br /><br />And I love the way it ended. Not just because Katie is finally starting to feel accepted into her own home, but because she learns that she's not alone. I love watching her ace detective skills. And seeing her connect with people that are much like herself.<br /><br /><b>2015</b><br />I read this book so many times when I was a kid. I think that it sparked my love for paranormal fiction. I loved the whole concept of a strange girl with telekinetic powers who didn't quite fit in anywhere. Then she finds out that there is a small group of kids that are just like her. She starts to seek them out, but then she realizes that someone is trying to track her down. It's a well-written novel with memorable characters and I would absolutely recommend it to younger readers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5821762741146450609" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3814964869042340255" style="display:none">First of all: holy crap. Dave sent this to me out of the blue, and when I pulled it out of the bubble envelope, I almost jumped. A real icy blast from the past.<br /><br />But by way of direct review: loved it when I was a kid, loved it again. Had to stay up and read it even though I didn't collect it from the mail until last thing at night. I definitely read it with more of a sinister bent than a kids' book warrants, but I like it better that way.<br /><br />A girl with silver eyes and mysterious powers (telekinesis and a little telepathy) discovers there may be other children just like her. Since she has always had trouble fitting in, she seeks them out--but discovers there are people seeking her out as well. <br /><br />I remember thinking when I was a kid that it shouldn't have ended so neatly; maybe if I have time, I'll write the final chapter that should have been. I'm pretty sure Willo Roberts had other ideas about how it should have gone--something a little more Robert Cormier, perhaps?</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer138351221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10431186645684418293">I loved this book when I was a kid, so I was happy to find it stood up to adult reading. It's refreshing to find a tale of psychic children who use their powers not to save the world but to make their beds, harass their babysitters, and generally <i>act like kids</i>. The conflict arises from misunderstandings between kids and adults, not some evil opposing force. <br/><br/>In short, there's no "with great power comes great responsibility" here, just good old-fashioned fun. And it is old-fashioned in a certain s</span>
  <span id="freeText10431186645684418293" style="display:none">I loved this book when I was a kid, so I was happy to find it stood up to adult reading. It's refreshing to find a tale of psychic children who use their powers not to save the world but to make their beds, harass their babysitters, and generally <i>act like kids</i>. The conflict arises from misunderstandings between kids and adults, not some evil opposing force. <br /><br />In short, there's no "with great power comes great responsibility" here, just good old-fashioned fun. And it is old-fashioned in a certain sense--the book is very definitively set in the late 1970s, with fashion and child-rearing attitudes reflecting the time period. But I think it would still be enjoyable for a modern elementary or middle school student.<br /><br />My memory had the book running much longer, so I was surprised to see it ended where it did. Pity the author never wrote a sequel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10431186645684418293" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_700598402" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2251084125070277238">This is one of those books that I was told to read back in school by other kids who knew I loved to read. I'd always intended to pick this book up, and even now when I mentioned on Facebook that I was reading it, I had a handful of comments about how this was someone's favorite book growing up.<br/><br/>Here too, is a book that needs to be examined in context of the time.<br/><br/>Back when this was written in 1980 there wasn't much that would be considered paranormal for kids to read. So the idea of a girl with th</span>
  <span id="freeText2251084125070277238" style="display:none">This is one of those books that I was told to read back in school by other kids who knew I loved to read. I'd always intended to pick this book up, and even now when I mentioned on Facebook that I was reading it, I had a handful of comments about how this was someone's favorite book growing up.<br /><br />Here too, is a book that needs to be examined in context of the time.<br /><br />Back when this was written in 1980 there wasn't much that would be considered paranormal for kids to read. So the idea of a girl with the powers of telekinesis is pretty cool overall, and her situation is exciting. <br /><br />So from that point of view, had I read this book back when I was ten, I would probably have been floored and thought this the most awesome book ever.<br /><br />What bothered me is something I see when I compare it to work of today, where paranormal takes such outlandish forms, and telekinesis seems a little bit...tame. To be fair the story is still exciting, at least for a little while. But the ending comes so quickly and just kind of...drops...with the feeling that the REAL story is yet to come...left me disappointed. Mostly because there IS NO SEQUEL to let us know what happens from here, when the story was just starting to get so interesting. <br /><br />For a book that I wanted to love very much, I came away a bit saddened that I hadn't read this at the age of ten so I can have that cherished memory as well. As it is, I'm glad I'm read it finally. But now I'm left wanting the rest of the story....</span>
  <a data-text-id="2251084125070277238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer933396929376441553">My sister had a copy of this book when we were kids and she read it over and over again. I don't know why I never read it. I typically read anything and everything that was lying around the house. Maybe Sis never let go of it long enough for me to read it.<br/><br/>I knew that the book involved a little girl who could move things with her mind. Sometimes I tried to do that too. I never had any success, but I halfway believed I couldn't do it because I didn't <i>believe</i> I could do it.<br/><br/>When I decided to read no</span>
  <span id="freeText933396929376441553" style="display:none">My sister had a copy of this book when we were kids and she read it over and over again. I don't know why I never read it. I typically read anything and everything that was lying around the house. Maybe Sis never let go of it long enough for me to read it.<br /><br />I knew that the book involved a little girl who could move things with her mind. Sometimes I tried to do that too. I never had any success, but I halfway believed I couldn't do it because I didn't <i>believe</i> I could do it.<br /><br />When I decided to read nothing but children's and YA fiction in January, I knew I had to read this one.<br /><br />Now I understand why my sis read it over and over again. This book is good stuff! It has everything a girl in elementary school could want--spunky, smart, clever, brave ten-year-old girl protagonist; danger; excitement, intrigue; under-involved parents; maginc/supernormal powers.<br /><br />I really enjoyed reading this book, and I'm sorry that I waited so long to do so.<br /><br />Update: I'm adding this book to my "found" shelf because I helped someone on the What's the Name of This Book??? group find it. Here's the link to the thread<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/810886-ya-book-a-bunch-of-psychic-kids-have-a-reunion?type=topic#comment_45728999">http://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/8...</a></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35927407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14482362972866166183">This book made a huge impression on me when I was a kid, and I've since learned that many other people remember it fondly. I was pleased to find that it holds up pretty well on re-reading, although it's shorter and less in-depth than I remembered -- I apparently inadvertantly mixed in some plot elements from Stephen King's <i>Firestarter</i> in my recollection.<br/><br/>Anyway, like I said, it holds up well. Katie, who has psychokinetic powers, remains a wonderfully precocious and slightly off-putting protagonis</span>
  <span id="freeText14482362972866166183" style="display:none">This book made a huge impression on me when I was a kid, and I've since learned that many other people remember it fondly. I was pleased to find that it holds up pretty well on re-reading, although it's shorter and less in-depth than I remembered -- I apparently inadvertantly mixed in some plot elements from Stephen King's <i>Firestarter</i> in my recollection.<br /><br />Anyway, like I said, it holds up well. Katie, who has psychokinetic powers, remains a wonderfully precocious and slightly off-putting protagonist; even as the reader sympathizes and identifies with her, there is still a touch of the alien, which is fantastic. I think this might be one of Roberts' best, in terms of characters, but I haven't read all that much Willo Davis Roberts, so I could be wrong. The book appears to be out of print, which is unfortunate; it didn't strike me as particularly dated, and I think that it could easily sit comfortably on the shelf next to some of the other popular science fiction middle readers at my library.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14482362972866166183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1375600426" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1375600426" />
    <a title="Brenda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2197013-brenda"><img alt="Brenda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603310920p2/2197013.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1375600426?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenda" href="/user/show/2197013-brenda">Brenda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780689307867" href="/book/show/1179779.The_Girl_with_the_Silver_Eyes">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Brenda by: </span>
      The Midnight Garden Classic Read-along
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2197013-brenda?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1375600426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8120264217272103156">Originally posted at <a href='http://www.logcabinlibrary.blogspot.com/2015/08/classic-mg-read-along-girl-with-silver.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Log Cabin Library</a><br/><br/>When Katie Welker was four, her parents got divorced and she began living with her grandmother. But, now that her grandmother is gone, she has moved to the city to live with her mother. Katie has always made people feel nervous, even her family. Maybe it was her silver eyes, or the way that she never expressed any emotion on her face. Or maybe it was because they suspected that she was peculiar and strange things always happened when she was around. Objects </span>
  <span id="freeText8120264217272103156" style="display:none">Originally posted at <a href="http://www.logcabinlibrary.blogspot.com/2015/08/classic-mg-read-along-girl-with-silver.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Log Cabin Library</a><br /><br />When Katie Welker was four, her parents got divorced and she began living with her grandmother. But, now that her grandmother is gone, she has moved to the city to live with her mother. Katie has always made people feel nervous, even her family. Maybe it was her silver eyes, or the way that she never expressed any emotion on her face. Or maybe it was because they suspected that she was peculiar and strange things always happened when she was around. Objects would move, breezes seemed to appear out of nowhere, doors slammed and papers scattered unexpectedly. But, people didn't know that Katie had always been able to move these things with her mind. She'd tried to blend in, seem normal, but it can be exhausting pretending to be normal all of the time. Now that Katie is in the city, she's faced with her mother trying to get her a sitter and a strange man who is asking a lot of questions about her. Katie also uncovers that there were three other children born during the same time as her who might be just as peculiar as she is, and she plans to find them.<br /><br /> I enjoyed reading The Girl with the Silver Eyes, I can't say that I've read many books where the main character has telekinetic powers and telepathy. Well, Carrie does come to mind, but this is no way near that book. The writing style defiantly had a classic feel to it for me. A sort of gentler time, where your next door neighbor (Mrs. M) is looking in on you. I'm not convinced that this book would work as well now as it might have back then. There isn't nearly enough action and I so wish there had been more about Katie's powers. There are a few humorous moments where she pulls pranks on her disgruntled neighbor Mr. H. Like when he doesn't pay the newspaper boy and I did really like Katie's ability to tell what Lobo (Mrs. M's cat) was thinking. There were also lots of questions that I was able to ponder with my child as I was reading. For example, Katie questions whether having telekinesis makes her life easier. Sure it would be cool to swirl leaves around you as you walked, but does putting sugar in your tea with your mind make things easier? When would these powers be most useful? Also, would it be better to know an animals or peoples thoughts? Katie ponders each of these as she is learning more about whether her peculiarities are due to something that happened to her, versus due to a freak of nature. What got me the most is how alone Katie feels, not accepted by her family and having no friends. And what it means to want to be accepted for who you are. Although, I was happy for Katie when she finally connected with the other children, I couldn't help feeling like the ending wasn't completely resolved for me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8120264217272103156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1093634662" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1093634662" />
    <a title="Pili" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9540219-pili"><img alt="Pili" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403408394p2/9540219.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1093634662?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pili" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pili" href="/user/show/9540219-pili">Pili</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9540219-pili?shelf=the-midnight-garden-classic-ya-mg">the-midnight-garden-classic-ya-mg</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9540219-pili?shelf=105-challenge-middle-grade">105-challenge-middle-grade</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1093634662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15273172942135964482">This month I read the #tmgreadalong book on time and I really enjoyed it! <br/>I keep on thinking how much I would have liked to have read this one as a young girl and how I would have dreamed that I could also move things with my mind, like Katie does.<br/><br/>Katie has just moved into a new place with her mum and she's having to adapt to the new place and living with her mother and a new place and new people. Something that might be hard for any kid, but Katie has never been a normal kid, and adults tend to</span>
  <span id="freeText15273172942135964482" style="display:none">This month I read the #tmgreadalong book on time and I really enjoyed it! <br />I keep on thinking how much I would have liked to have read this one as a young girl and how I would have dreamed that I could also move things with my mind, like Katie does.<br /><br />Katie has just moved into a new place with her mum and she's having to adapt to the new place and living with her mother and a new place and new people. Something that might be hard for any kid, but Katie has never been a normal kid, and adults tend to be wary of her.<br /><br />She finds herself dealing with unwanted babysitters, nice neighbours and others that are certified jerks and she makes some friends, and finds some acceptance to her oddity but still finds herself very much alone. <br /><br />It was hard for me to read about how alone Katie really was, not able to confide in her mother or having her support, but instead feel like she was judged weird and held at arms lenght. She entertained herself and even if she tried to hide how she was "peculiar", she still felt like she shouldn't have to be ashamed of it.<br /><br />I loved to see how resourceful she was once she found a hint of answers for her being different and how determined she was to finding someone else that would maybe be like her and would understand how she felt.<br /><br />Seeing Katie use her powers for both good and mischief was a lot of fun, and I kept rooting for her to get rid of the babysitters and instead hang out with her neighbour, who would lend her books and was kind and understanding for a change. And once more silver eyes enter the fray, it was a lot of fun to see them understanding each other and working together.<br /><br />It was quiter heartwarming seeing Katie's mum proving herself reliable and a better mother figure than appearances led us to believe at first, even if something dramatic had to happen to shake her into it.<br /><br />All in all, a great read, both fun and with enough depth to hold its own well with younger and older readers alike! Well deserved 4 stars!</span>
  <a data-text-id="15273172942135964482" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/270791923" />
    <a title="Alice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1247026-alice"><img alt="Alice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1285199742p2/1247026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/270791923?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice" href="/user/show/1247026-alice">Alice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780808545255" href="/book/show/1179777.Girl_with_the_Silver_Eyes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=ya-mg-kids">ya-mg-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1247026-alice?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer270791923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer864461992472944719">I read this book when I was younger, and I remember liking it very much, but I couldn't remember why.<br/><br/>Katie Welker is an unusual girl. She creeps people out, because of her flat expression and silver-colored eyes. That's even before they know that she can move small objects with her mind, call up breezes that can slam doors and scatter papers, and she can communicate with animals. When her grandmother, who's been taking care of her most of her life, passes away unexpectedly, she goes to live with</span>
  <span id="freeText864461992472944719" style="display:none">I read this book when I was younger, and I remember liking it very much, but I couldn't remember why.<br /><br />Katie Welker is an unusual girl. She creeps people out, because of her flat expression and silver-colored eyes. That's even before they know that she can move small objects with her mind, call up breezes that can slam doors and scatter papers, and she can communicate with animals. When her grandmother, who's been taking care of her most of her life, passes away unexpectedly, she goes to live with her mother. Soon after, a man shows up asking odd questions about her, and she finds out she's not alone in what makes her different from so many others.<br /><br />It's like the book was written for a young me. Young Katie is never happier than when she's curled up reading an excellent book. Her classmates don't like her and make fun of her, but she has ways of deterring bullies, though the bullies don't entirely realize what she can do. And her happy ending is finding other kids just like her.<br /><br />But this could just as easily be an allegory for autism. While kids on the spectrum don't move things with their mind, they do tend to be shunned by their peers and have a sense of not fitting in, and there's been a debate, as at the end of this book, about whether they're better off sheltered from the general population or integrated into society. (We're moving more toward a model of integration, which is why I'm glad the book didn't end up with Katie and her newfound friends being shuttled off to an institution. The ending leaves it open to interpretation.)<br /><br />Overall, while the writing style doesn't hold up to what I remembered, I did enjoy refreshing my memory about this book. The story held up well, and it told me a lot about my younger self, who loved this book to pieces.</span>
  <a data-text-id="864461992472944719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1344434272" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1344434272" />
    <a title="Katharine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32689195-katharine"><img alt="Katharine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564947418p2/32689195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1344434272?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Katharine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Katharine" href="/user/show/32689195-katharine">Katharine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1344434272" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15577586351234294437">The Girl With the Silver Eyes is based on a similar idea as Roald Dahl's Matilda, that is: what would happen if a little girl had a telekinetic ability? <br/><br/>What I liked about The Girl with the Silver Eyes is that it was focused more on normal everyday type circumstances and people finding connection in their differences rather than focusing primarily on the fantastic. <br/>In Matilda, the heroine suffers misunderstanding on all sides, and exacts childish revenge on her tormenters. She is put against a f</span>
  <span id="freeText15577586351234294437" style="display:none">The Girl With the Silver Eyes is based on a similar idea as Roald Dahl's Matilda, that is: what would happen if a little girl had a telekinetic ability? <br /><br />What I liked about The Girl with the Silver Eyes is that it was focused more on normal everyday type circumstances and people finding connection in their differences rather than focusing primarily on the fantastic. <br />In Matilda, the heroine suffers misunderstanding on all sides, and exacts childish revenge on her tormenters. She is put against a fantastical villain, and must save the day. <br />Katie is similarly clever and deals with misunderstanding as Matilda does, but she overcomes her obstacles by finding friends who will accept her for who she is, and deals with events which feel more grounded in reality, her strange abilities seeming more believable because of the normalcy around her. <br /><br />I liked The Girl with the Silver Eyes best for its message of being different yet discovering you are not alone in being different. I think Matilda did not get a chance to see this, as she was so set apart from everyone. <br /><br />I did like Matilda's ending better than Katie's though. Matilda's was a fully circled end, she went from being unloved to finding someone who loved her; whereas although Katie found others like her that would understand her, her ending felt very untied. We were left with the adults in her life settling her fate, and not told what they decided. This unfinished end was a frustrating conclusion to a otherwise charming tale.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15577586351234294437" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_31884666" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31884666" />
    <a title="Swankivy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84660-swankivy"><img alt="Swankivy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220079335p2/84660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/31884666?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Swankivy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Swankivy" href="/user/show/84660-swankivy">Swankivy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer31884666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5048865273711587221">I'm gonna guess this was one of the first books I read that featured a main character who lived in the modern, ordinary world but had extraordinary abilities. That's what I liked about it, and it was probably my first exposure to a fictional character with telekinetic powers. ::sniffle--ahh, the memories!:: But you know what? The book itself is kinda one-dimensional. The characters are odd and they're in a situation that just seems really written and contrived. Katie's quest to find others like </span>
  <span id="freeText5048865273711587221" style="display:none">I'm gonna guess this was one of the first books I read that featured a main character who lived in the modern, ordinary world but had extraordinary abilities. That's what I liked about it, and it was probably my first exposure to a fictional character with telekinetic powers. ::sniffle--ahh, the memories!:: But you know what? The book itself is kinda one-dimensional. The characters are odd and they're in a situation that just seems really written and contrived. Katie's quest to find others like herself was kinda touching, and I definitely related to the feeling she had that nobody else was like her, but I didn't like her very much, and I thought everyone around her behaved in unrealistic ways, and even the way the author wrote about her powers was just weird and inconsistent. I wonder today if the novel series I wrote in college--whose main character is a girl born to ordinary parents who inexplicably turned out to have telekinetic powers--in some way owes its existence to my buried suspicion that I could do better than this. ;) </span>
  <a data-text-id="5048865273711587221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FL0x9APOa+UVNYkRk+MtuaNeVoYQpDYaL2klmSzS1kuby/q7z8ksWBtzoVBrHoYLNGmP1IdDJsNc4ONj+5FQdA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kUIeaORnNA4Q8MSBueoJHBxeU7dIbKVIasK//fRqeB8eNNUnKGBzsx627MBBF6Kui2mK5d+LtZEZS3kHIyn+IA==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/83396.Willo_Davis_Roberts">About Willo Davis Roberts</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/83396.Willo_Davis_Roberts"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1211816526p3/83396.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>

followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10761840794288226170">Willo Davis Roberts was an American writer chiefly known for her mystery novels for children and young adults. She won Edgar Allan Poe awards in 1989, 1995, and 1997 for best juvenile and best young adult mysteries. Her books included <i>The View from the Cherry Tree, Twisted Summer, Don't Hurt Laurie, Megan's Island, Baby-sitting is a Dangerous Job, Hostage, The Girl with Silver Eyes, The One Left B</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10761840794288226170" style="display:none">Willo Davis Roberts was an American writer chiefly known for her mystery novels for children and young adults. She won Edgar Allan Poe awards in 1989, 1995, and 1997 for best juvenile and best young adult mysteries. Her books included <i>The View from the Cherry Tree, Twisted Summer, Don't Hurt Laurie, Megan's Island, Baby-sitting is a Dangerous Job, Hostage, The Girl with Silver Eyes, The One Left Behind</i> and <i>Scared Stiff</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10761840794288226170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
